  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_6d163953-9709-4ddc-b3ae-e62377ec5264'
      });
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer9931250836963293370">Nearing her one-hundredth birthday, Roseanne McNulty faces an uncertain future, as the Roscommon Regional Mental hospital where she's spent the best part of her adult life prepares for closure. Over the weeks leading up to this upheaval, she talks often with her psychiatrist Dr Grene, and their relationship intensifies and complicates.<br/><br/>Told through their respective journals</span>
  <span id="freeText9931250836963293370" style="display:none">Nearing her one-hundredth birthday, Roseanne McNulty faces an uncertain future, as the Roscommon Regional Mental hospital where she's spent the best part of her adult life prepares for closure. Over the weeks leading up to this upheaval, she talks often with her psychiatrist Dr Grene, and their relationship intensifies and complicates.<br /><br />Told through their respective journals, the story that emerges is at once shocking and deeply beautiful. Refracted through the haze of memory and retelling, Roseanne's story becomes an alternative, secret history of Ireland's changing character and the story of a life blighted by terrible mistreatment and ignorance, and yet marked still by love and passion and hope.</span>

<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/1503133-i-haven-t-read-this-book-but-is-the">I haven&#39;t read this book, but is the character, Roseanne McNulty, an allegory for Ireland itself? That&#39;s what I gather from the synopsis. If answering would result in spoilers, then I guess I&#39;d rather not know.</a>
</div>

<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/13273233-carol-friel"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1349025551i/13273233._UX100_CR0,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/13273233-carol-friel">Carol Friel</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">The book certainly presents various characteristics - unpleasant ones - that may be typical of the Irish at a certain period of time (the 20th century<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">The book certainly presents various characteristics - unpleasant ones - that may be typical of the Irish at a certain period of time (the 20th century). <br /> I would not call Roseanne an allegory of Ireland but would call her a representative of those who have suffered from the prejudice and judgmentalism that have been somewhat rampant in Irish history and the Irish character.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1237196-petra-x?shelf=2015-reviews">2015-reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1237196-petra-x?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1237196-petra-x?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1267993233" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13346175052546878997">Sexuality in beautiful young women in backward societies is a double-edged sword. On the one hand it attracts young men, sometimes into marriage, and on the other it can seem to justify the accusation of being called a slut. And should the woman have a baby outside marriage, then the accusation is proved and the girl condemned and if punishment follows, it will be considered validated.<br/><br/>It's not much different today, is it? Call a girl a slut and people look at her askance. Not a nice person, not </span>
  <span id="freeText13346175052546878997" style="display:none">Sexuality in beautiful young women in backward societies is a double-edged sword. On the one hand it attracts young men, sometimes into marriage, and on the other it can seem to justify the accusation of being called a slut. And should the woman have a baby outside marriage, then the accusation is proved and the girl condemned and if punishment follows, it will be considered validated.<br /><br />It's not much different today, is it? Call a girl a slut and people look at her askance. Not a nice person, not someone you'd want to mix with. But today the punishment is generally only social exclusion from (hypocritical) social groups. Back then, in the time of this book after the Civil War in Ireland, it could mean being locked up in mental home for the rest of their lives.<br /><br />And now the girl, the woman, the old lady who is a century old is telling her tale to her doctor. He is a gentle, understanding, unfulfilled man who is doing his best for his patients who now these old institutions are being dissolved, must re-enter the world or adapt to a modern mental home.<br /><br />He listens to Roseanne's story, he asks people about her, and discovers a document that tells a different story from her conversation. But there is still another story, the one she is writing of her life and hiding in the floorboards. These stories and the doctor's intertwine and they both learn far more about each other than they ever could have suspected. <br /><br />It's quite an eye-opening ending, and in retrospect that is what the story is leading to all along, but I found it too pat. I thought the story of Roseanne and the Troubles with all the violence and wickedness of those timeswere good enough to stand on their own without a contrived conclusion. So 4 stars. 4.5 stars. Ok, I really enjoyed it. 5 stars!</span>
  <a data-text-id="13346175052546878997" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1421486732" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1421486732" />
    <a title="Candi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3639005-candi"><img alt="Candi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585187464p2/3639005.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1421486732?book_show_action=true">Mar 13, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Candi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Candi" href="/user/show/3639005-candi">Candi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/6530484-the-secret-scripture">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3639005-candi?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3639005-candi?shelf=european-literature">european-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3639005-candi?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1421486732" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6444352105027211509"><i>"For history as far as I can see is not the arrangement of what happens, in sequence and in truth, but a fabulous arrangement of surmises and guesses held up as a banner against the assault of withering truth."</i><br/><br/><b>The Secret Scripture</b> is a sublime work of fiction about memory and its effect on history and truth. It’s about love and loss, grief, religion and Ireland. It nearly broke my heart, but left me with a glimpse of joy and hope. It’s a slow unraveling of the mystery surrounding the reason why </span>
  <span id="freeText6444352105027211509" style="display:none"><i>"For history as far as I can see is not the arrangement of what happens, in sequence and in truth, but a fabulous arrangement of surmises and guesses held up as a banner against the assault of withering truth."</i><br /><br /><b>The Secret Scripture</b> is a sublime work of fiction about memory and its effect on history and truth. It’s about love and loss, grief, religion and Ireland. It nearly broke my heart, but left me with a glimpse of joy and hope. It’s a slow unraveling of the mystery surrounding the reason why Roseanne McNulty has been institutionalized at the Roscommon Regional Mental Hospital for the past sixty years of her nearly one hundred years of life. Her story is gradually revealed through her own narrative as recorded in a hidden journal as well as through inquiries made by Dr. Grene, the psychiatrist charged with her care. Roscommon is slated to shut down and Dr. Grene must determine which patients, if any, were wrongfully committed for reasons other than mental instability. The point of view alternates between Roseanne’s voice and that of Dr. Grene. What Roseanne tells us and what Dr. Grene uncovers from old documents are two different versions of “the truth”. Dr. Grene must determine which to believe and how these stories ultimately matter in his own decision regarding Roseanne’s fate in her old age. <i>"The one thing that is fatal in the reading of impromptu history is a wrongful desire for accuracy. There is no such thing."</i><br /><br />Roseanne’s story is a tragic one and my heart ached for this gentle soul left abandoned due to the ignorance and prejudices of other human beings. A Protestant in a country ravaged by civil war, Roseanne is a victim of the power of the Irish Catholic Church in the early 1900s. Father Gaunt is a symbol of the perversion of the influences of the church at that time over the lives and the moral judgments of those in its path. <i>"Morality has its own civil wars, with its own victims in their own time and place."</i><br /> <br />The story is told slowly and is one to be read with quiet contemplation, allowing Sebastian Barry’s extraordinary prose to wash over and captivate you. I closed the book with a feeling of absolute contentment despite the grim journey. I will no doubt read more of this author’s work and am in fact anxious to do so. I highly recommend this five-star book. <br /><br /><i>"There are things that move at a human pace before our eyes, but other things move in arcs so great they are as good as invisible."</i><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6444352105027211509" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9570782450843346757">A superbly crafted novel told in under 300 pages. A story so cleverly written that for half of the Novel I believed I was reading a non fiction account. <br/>image: <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1491145527i/22380104._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><i>Rosanne Mc Nulty is nearing her hundredth birthday in the mental hospital where she was committed as a young woman. A story so cleverly written that for half of the Novel I believed I was reading a non fiction account. <br>image: <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1491145527i/22380104._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><i>Rosanne Mc Nulty is nearing her hundredth birthday in the mental hospital where she was committed as a young woman. While Dr Green investigates, Roseanne looks back on the tragedies and passions she has locked away in her secret journal, from her turbulent rural childhood to the marriage she believed would bring her happiness. But when Dr Green finally uncovers the circumstances of her arrival at the hospital it leads to a shocking secret.</i><br><br>I have read several of Barry's novels to date and this one is one of my all time favourites as this story is moving and haunting and Barry has a talent at capturing an Irishiness that is real and authentic of the time and place.<br><br>On reading this for a second time as the first time I read it was in 2009 and since the movie is out at the moment I wanted to refresh my memory and see if I would enjoy the reading experience all over again. On finishing the Novel I don't think I want to actually see the movie now as I am afraid it will spoil the vivid images created in my mind by Barry's glorious writing.<br><br>Quote from book: <i>my mother’s wits were now in an attic of her head which had neither door nor stair, or at least none that I could find.” </i><br><br>This is a moving story about memory and conflicting versions of the past, beautiful and haunting and yet uplifting. A well deserved 5 stars and without doubt it earned its place on the short list for the Man Booker Prize in 2008.</span>

<div id="review_1949482930" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1949482930" />
    <a title="Diane Barnes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7262060-diane-barnes"><img alt="Diane Barnes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1329773692p2/7262060.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1949482930?book_show_action=true">Apr 17, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diane Barnes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diane Barnes" href="/user/show/7262060-diane-barnes">Diane Barnes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7262060-diane-barnes?shelf=motley-crew">motley-crew</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1949482930" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7719379243902858078">Last month my book club read Sebastian Barry's "Days Without End", and we all loved it unconditionally. That almost never happens. So our hostess up for the April read decided to assign another of Barry's books, although she had some reservations that it might compare unfavorably to the one we thought so highly of. How can it possibly be as good, she asked?<br/>She needn't have worried, because it was as good, but in a different way. The language was still soaring and poetic, the characters just as s</span>
  <span id="freeText7719379243902858078" style="display:none">Last month my book club read Sebastian Barry's "Days Without End", and we all loved it unconditionally. That almost never happens. So our hostess up for the April read decided to assign another of Barry's books, although she had some reservations that it might compare unfavorably to the one we thought so highly of. How can it possibly be as good, she asked?<br />She needn't have worried, because it was as good, but in a different way. The language was still soaring and poetic, the characters just as soulful, and the story.....! What a story!<br />The tale is told through two journals, one by the psychiatrist needing to assess an elderly patient before the insane asylum she has been in for 60 years is demolished, and the other by the patient herself, 100 year old Roseanne Clear (or McNulty, depending on whose story can be believed.) There is also a brief deposition by the Catholic priest responsible for her incarceration, and for many of her woes, and I sincerely hope there is a special place in Hell reserved for him.<br />This is a multi-layered novel, with little bits of truth and understanding poking through every once in a while. Even so, I had to read the climax four times just to believe it. I still get chills thinking about it.<br />Even though the setting is from 1907 - 2007, it reads like a Victorian novel, maybe because of the handwritten journals and the Irish locales.<br />This was a complete departure from some of my other recent reading, and I was completely swept up in the story. So much so that I continue to wish Father Gaunt consigned to that special place in Hell.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7719379243902858078" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13526883892254990775"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1484262138i/21701413._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/><b>Are you an honest person? Truly?</b><br/><br/>Perhaps you instinctively think “Yes”, even as you realise you are not always scrupulously so, often for the best of reasons. Often. But not always. <br/>One can’t be totally honest all the time, can one? Can one?<br/>What is “truth” anyway, but a social construct?!<br/><br/>“<i>What's wrong about her account if she sincerely believes it?</i>”<br/>“<i>There is no factual truth.</i>”<br/>It matters more that the person is “<i>admirable, living, and complete</i>” - what a curious trio of adjectives.<br/><br/>In a post-truth e</span>
  <span id="freeText13526883892254990775" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1484262138i/21701413._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>Are you an honest person? Truly?</b><br><br>Perhaps you instinctively think “Yes”, even as you realise you are not always scrupulously so, often for the best of reasons. Often. But not always. <br>One can’t be totally honest all the time, can one? Can one?<br>What is “truth” anyway, but a social construct?!<br><br>“<i>What's wrong about her account if she sincerely believes it?</i>”<br>“<i>There is no factual truth.</i>”<br>It matters more that the person is “<i>admirable, living, and complete</i>” - what a curious trio of adjectives.<br><br>In a post-truth era, on a big day for possibly “fake news” (a euphemism for lies and propaganda), our collective ability to recognise truth slips ever further from our grasp. <br><br><b>Stories</b><br><br>This is stor<b>ies</b> of centenarian Roseanne’s liv<b>es</b>. The tides of two world wars and a civil war bring opportunity, fear, birth, death, deceit, despair, and change - crashing, crushing, on the shores of Sligo. <br><br>She’s approaching her 100th birthday, and has been in asylums for around seventy years. Dr Grene has to gently uncover Roseanne’s story to see if she should move to a new, smaller institution or if the truth will set her “free” for care in the community (a term he knows is inaccurate). <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1484262138i/21701414._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>This isn’t really about madness versus sanity (though it’s an issue for many characters) or even incarceration. It’s about telling stories – to <b>hide</b> the truth as well as to <b>reveal</b> it. <br><br>Roseanne and Dr Grene are both writing accounts of the past, especially Roseanne’s past, in part to avoid considering the future. Each is unaware the other is doing so. <br><br>The reader experiences layers of contradiction, distance and distortion from the passage of time, deep trauma, and efforts to protect from shame or guilt.<br><br>And then there is a third written testimony, from Fr Gaunt, and remnants of official records (“A little apocryphal gospel”) which readers get second hand via Dr Grene, and which are further muddied when the doctor realises he’s filling in gaps that Fr Gaunt did not. Another layer of embroidery.<br><br>And what about the unknown hand who brought all the narratives together? How do we untangle the truth? Which version of the tower and feathers and hammers is true? Could it even be both?!<br><br><b>Why are they writing?</b><br>No plot spoilers, just background notes and detail. <br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br>•	Roseanne wants “an honest-minded history of myself” because “My secrets are my fortune and my sanity.” She has experienced the dire consequences of gossip and presumption more than once, so “There must be accuracy and rightness.”<br><br>•	Dr Grene’s writing is an extension of his work, a distraction from personal loss, and “a sign of ongoing inner life” that triggers ideas and insight.<br><br>•	Fr Gaunt’s “desire... to tell the story illuminates it. He is unburdening himself, as he might a sin.”<br><br>A person without stories that outlive them becomes lost to family, lost to history, “sad black names on within family trees, with half a date dangling after and a question mark”.<br>Roseanne’s father relished telling stories from his life, but mother is “singularly without stories” and eventually mute. She vanishes from the story. <br> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br><b>Words</b><br>No plot spoilers, just background notes and detail. <br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br>The writing it beautiful and lyrical (see quotes below, especially about sea, ran, weather, and light), but some words have special weight. The pages are infused with religious people, language, and symbols (a priest, the church, visions that might be angels, and roast lamb in a sacrificial context).<br><br>•	Is “Roseanne’s Testimony of herself” her title or someone else’s?<br>•	Is “secret scripture” an oxymoron – and who calls it that?<br>•	What is sacred about it – the story or the telling of it? <br>•	Dr Grene’s record is merely his “Commonplace Book”.<br><br>Perhaps because she has been deemed unclean by some, Roseanne notes cleanliness a great deal:<br>•	“My father was the cleanest man in all the Christian world.”<br>•	Father Gaunt is “cleaner than the daylight moon” and later, his “cleanliness made me fearful”.<br>•	Dr Grene sits on Roseanne’s bed “as if it were the cleanest bed in all Christendom”.<br>•	And she only writes her notes on clean sheets of paper. <br><br>Names may tell us something:<br>•	Clear is the surname of Roseanne’s family, but their history is opaque.<br>•	Grene, the doctor’s surname, is an important colour, as well as being associated with Ireland generally. <br>•	Gaunt can never be good, can it?<br>•	Roseanne loves flowers, especially roses, as does Dr Grene’s wife, and when he visits Roseanne’s old house, he finds and plucks a rose.<br><br>Roses are not the only hybrids. It’s significant that some people are part English and part Irish.<br> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br><b>Poisoned Chalices</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1484262138i/21701415._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Female beauty and sexuality are poisoned chalices in a society where only women are shamed and punished for the consequences of both. Mere existence “Caused him… displeasure and disquiet at the nature of a woman.”<br><br><b>What Matters in The End?</b><br><br>I guessed the main twist (and others) well before it was revealed. I didn’t mind. But what <b>did</b> dilute the book’s power was the rushed but detailed explanation of the complex chain of events, involving many people, that made it possible. Far better had it been chance (fate) or merely unexplained. More credible, too. <br><br>If one strives to be the architect of one’s own life, it’s not much of a stretch to be the curator of one’s own history, editing a little along the way, is it?<br><br>And if you trust or blame fate instead, perhaps you have even more cause to write yourself a happier beginning, middle, and end. <br><br>I ask again: <b>Are you an honest person? Truly? Is anyone?</b><br><br><b>Quotes</b><br><br>No plot spoilers, but hidden for brevity and easy scrolling. They’re worth the click, though. It’s not just the setting that is typically Irish, but the writing, too.<br><br><b>History, Memory, Truth, and Stories Quotes</b><br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br>•	“No one has the monopoly on truth.”<br><br>•	“History… is not the arrangement of what happened… but a fabulous arrangement of surmises and guesses held up as a banner against the assault of withering truth.” Fabulous in more than one sense.<br><br>•	“The written word assumes authority but it may not have it.”<br><br>•	“I thought others were the authors of my sort of misfortune.”<br><br>•	“A child is never the author of his own history.”<br><br>•	“I am only the midwife to my own old story.”<br><br>•	“Memory… if it is neglected becomes like a box room… the contents jumbled about.”<br><br>•	“Everything I remember… may not be <i>real</i>... There was so much turmoil… I took refuge in other impossible histories, in dreams, in fantasies? I don’t know.”<br><br> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br><b>Sanity and Madness Quotes</b><br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br>•	"Madness… has many flowers, rising from the same stem."<br><br>•	“Learned a sort of viral madness” is often the fate of long-term inmates of the asylum. The worse cases are “sleeping towards death, crawling on bleeding knees towards our Lord… I whisper a prayer to hurry up their souls to heaven.”<br><br>•	“In here, amid the shadows and the distant cries, the greatest virtue is silence.”<br><br>•	“Sane to a degree that makes sanity almost undesirable.”<br><br> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br><b>Beauty Quotes</b><br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br>•	 “A more beautiful girl Sligo never saw, she had skin soft as feathers” and “green eyes like American emeralds”. The sort of beauty “no man can protect himself against”. <br><br>•	“My mother suffered strangely under her halo of beauty.” <br><br>•	“Beautiful once, but beauty ended.”<br><br>•	“All that remains of me now is a rumour of beauty.”<br><br> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br><b>People and Relationships Quotes</b><br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br>•	 “He had that rare ability to let things ease in himself in the company of a child, and be stupid and gay in the parched light.”<br><br>•	“It is no crime to love your father.”<br><br>•	“My pride in her was my pride in myself.”<br><br>•	“Now we are two foreign countries and we simply have our embassies in the same house. Relations are friendly but strictly diplomatic. There is an underlying sense of rumour, of judgement, of memory, like two peoples that have once committed grave crimes against each other, but in another generation.” <br><br>•	“We have neglected the tiny sentences of life and now the big ones are beyond our reach.” <br><br> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br><b>Sea, Rain, Weather, and Light Quotes</b><br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br>Humans came from the sea and therefore still long for it. Many characters walk the shore for peace or escape, and one is dramatically rescued from drowning. <br><br>•	“The rain drives everyone indoors and history with it… our world in its inner truth is so wet, the surprised greens of the fields and hills seem to burn with a sort of bewilderment, a wonderment.”<br><br>•	“Always the deluge of rain falling... making the houses shiver and huddle like people at a football match. Falling fantastically, in enormous amounts, the contents of a hundred rivers. And the river itself... swelling up, the beautiful swans taken by surprise, riding the torrent, being swept down under the bridge and reappearing the other side like unsuccessful suicides, their mysterious eyes shocked and black, their mysterious grace unassailed.”<br><br>•	“Sideways spring sunlight, that seemed to have crept in through the window-glass with an almost apologetic delicacy.”<br><br>•	“The wind was dancing about… so you didn’t know where the rain would catch you.”<br><br>•	“Rain striking the surface of the road and leaping about with a sort of anger.”<br><br>•	“The rain was like huge skirts, swirling and lifting.”<br><br>•	“The galloping sound of the sea, as it rushed in eagerly to take the empty places in its arms.”<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1484262138i/21701416.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>The famous Metal Man is “Solid and eternal… faithfully and stoically pointing down into the deep water.”<br><br> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br><b>Other Quotes</b><br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br>•	 “A cold town. Even the mountains stood away.”<br><br>•	“Any effort at gardening… is an effort to drag to earth the colours and importances of heaven.”<br><br>•	“Grief is two years long.” So says Fr Gaunt. <br><br>•	“My head was aflame with the deep dark pulse of grief, that beats like a physical pain, like a rat got into your brains, a rat on fire.”<br><br>•	“At close of day the ship we sail in is the soul, not the body.”<br><br>•	“His veritable gospel was Religio Medici by Sir Thomas Browne.”<br><br>•	“Slight as a watercolour, a mere gesture of bones and features.”<br><br>•	Café women “like veritable hens in a yard… the chat and gossip rising from them like dust from a desert caravan of camels.”<br><br>•	“Accents like beer bottles being smashed in a back lane.”<br><br> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br><b>Image Sources</b><br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br>Hand on Bible:<br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://goo.gl/images/52IiZm">https://goo.gl/images/52IiZm</a> <br>Poisoned chalice:<br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener Man, Coney Island, near Sligo:<br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" spoiler)</a>]</span></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>

<div id="review_2331791731" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2331791731?book_show_action=true">Mar 19, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cheri" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cheri" href="/user/show/106692-cheri">Cheri</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/6530484-the-secret-scripture">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=ireland">ireland</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=ireland-historical-fiction">ireland-historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=2018">2018</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=irish-author">irish-author</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2331791731" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2735695642783981483"><br/><i>
  <b>Fare thee well sweet Anna Liffy <br/>I can no longer stay <br/>And watch me new glass cages <br/>that spring up along me Ouay <br/>My mind's too full of memories <br/>too old to hear new chimes <br/>l'm a part of what was Dublin <br/>in the rare ould times.</b>
</i><br/>	--“Dublin in the Rare Ould Times,” Dublin City Ramblers, Songwriters: Pete St. John for the Dublin City Ramblers <br/><br/><br/><b>”Roseanne’s Testimony of Herself<br/><br/>“(<i>Patient, Roscommon Regional Mental Hospital, 1957 - )”</i></b><br/><br/><i>“The world begins anew with every birth, my father used to say. He forgo</i></span>
  <span id="freeText2735695642783981483" style="display:none"><br /><i>
  <b>Fare thee well sweet Anna Liffy <br />I can no longer stay <br />And watch me new glass cages <br />that spring up along me Ouay <br />My mind's too full of memories <br />too old to hear new chimes <br />l'm a part of what was Dublin <br />in the rare ould times.</b>
</i><br />	--“Dublin in the Rare Ould Times,” Dublin City Ramblers, Songwriters: Pete St. John for the Dublin City Ramblers <br /><br /><br /><b>”Roseanne’s Testimony of Herself<br /><br />“(<i>Patient, Roscommon Regional Mental Hospital, 1957 - )”</i></b><br /><br /><i>“The world begins anew with every birth, my father used to say. He forgot to say, with every death it ends. Or did not think he needed to. Because for a goodly part of his life he worked in a graveyard.”<br /><br />“The terror and hurt in my story happened because when I was young I thought others were the authors of my fortune or misfortune; I did not know that a person could hold up a wall made of imaginary bricks and mortar against the horrors and cruel, dark tricks of time that assail us, and be the author therefore of themselves.”<br /><br />“I am an old, old woman now, I may be as much as a hundred, though I do not know, and no one knows. I am only a thing left over, a remnant woman, and I do not even look like a human being no more, but a scraggy stretch of skin and bone in a bleak skirt and blouse, and a canvas jacket, and I sit here in my niche like a songless robin—no, like a mouse that died under the hearthstone where it was warm and lies now like a mummy in the pyramids.”</i><br /><br />Roseanne McNulty has lived at the Roscommon Regional Mental hospital more years of her adult life than not, she sits and waits – but not impatiently – for whatever end awaits. In those hours she writes the story of her life in the hopes that after she’s gone someone will believe the truth of her words, finally.<br /><br />Her psychiatrist, Dr. Grene, keeps his own journal: <br /><br /><b>(”Dr. Grene’s Commonplace Book<br /><i>Senior Psychiatrist, Roscommon Regional Mental Hospital”)</i></b><br /><br />He contemplates the decline of the building that houses these patients, and of the need to move to a newer building, and his views on Roseanne with the new hospital not being large enough to house all of their current patients. He wants to make the best decision for her in her remaining time. Those patients who he feels were unfairly committed, can, will be “let go,” as there are fewer rooms in the new facility. <br /><br />It’s through their journals that their stories emerge, a story of those years that make up Roseanne’s life, how she came to be a patient in this place, but also a story of Ireland. Of the times. Ignorance. War. Conflict. Love. Passion. Family. Loss. Grief. Religion. About memories and how our lives are built around them, and how they can come to haunt us. Perhaps sometimes they can save us, as well.<br /><br />This tale unravel slowly, as tales of a lifetime are wont to do, meandering a bit here and there through time and place, through the morals of the time, and the <i>music</i> - ah yes, the music - the places and people. It is a poignant one, her memories of her life with her father as a child, through her teenage years, her marriage, and on through the point where the whole story of Roseanne’s life finally comes to the surface, and we know her truth.<br /><br />Last fall I read Barry’s <i>‘Days Without End,’</i> and loved it, loved how beautifully written it was. Another beautiful, contemplative novel by this author, I am already looking forward to reading more of his works, but first I just want to dwell in this lovely moment and contemplate – or, as my grandfather used to say “sit a spell and ponder.”<br /> <br /><br />Recommended</span>

<div id="review_1845239404" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1845239404" />
    <a title="Debra" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5032725-debra"><img alt="Debra" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1525132364p2/5032725.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1845239404?book_show_action=true">Dec 20, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Debra" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Debra" href="/user/show/5032725-debra">Debra</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5032725-debra?shelf=favorite-books">favorite-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1845239404" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12838919517720373588">Rose McNultry is almost 100 years old. For most of her life she has been a patient in Roscommon Mental hospital in rural west Ireland. This "mad" woman has lived here most of her adult life. The hospital is going to be shut down and she is facing a scary future of being moved from where she has lived most of her life. She has frequent talks with her therapist/psychiatrist in the weeks leading up to the hospital's closure. Her therapists job is to determine what to do with the patients left behin</span>
  <span id="freeText12838919517720373588" style="display:none">Rose McNultry is almost 100 years old. For most of her life she has been a patient in Roscommon Mental hospital in rural west Ireland. This "mad" woman has lived here most of her adult life. The hospital is going to be shut down and she is facing a scary future of being moved from where she has lived most of her life. She has frequent talks with her therapist/psychiatrist in the weeks leading up to the hospital's closure. Her therapists job is to determine what to do with the patients left behind. He needs to determine who is of sound mind but institutionalized against their will and who is mentally ill. This also asks the question, if you are sane when you are institutionalized, will this prolonged treatment render you insane?<br /><br />Rose has been keeping a journal of her life which she keeps hidden under the floorboards of her room. She only takes her journals out when it is safe. It is through her journal entries that we learn about her past. Her relationship with her parents - she had a loving father and a mother who distances herself from her daughter. <br /><br />Rose falls in love with a young Man who has a domineering Mother who does not approve of her Catholic son being with a Presbyterian young woman. Father Gaunt makes sure that Rose does not marry the young man she is in love with. The priest’s misogyny, mistrust and dislike for women is Rose’s downfall. Ireland's history comes into play as does the Catholic church who "puts away" those are different, who are sexual or deemed "loose". A priest’s word is law back then and troublemakers are removed from society. As a result, tragedy, cruel treatment and prejudice ensues.<br /><br />I enjoyed how the story went back and forth telling Rose's story then and her story now. For some reason, the jumping back and forth between decades made Rose's story more sad - more poignant. I could feel her loneliness and pain. I, unlike Rose, would have been mad as hell at having been locked up all those years. I would have raged and fought. She chose the path of forgiveness. She is a survivor. There is something quite beautiful in her ability to sit with her loss and loneliness and forgive those who have wronged her. <br /><br />This book is beautifully written. It's a big book with a lot of heart. It has a very poetic and Gothic feel to it. For some reason, while reading this book, I thought of other Gothic books such as Jane Eyre. These books are not the same and do not have similar story lines, but they do both have a lot of atmosphere and have the same type of dreary feel to them.<br /><br />See more of my reviews at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.openbookpost.com">www.openbookpost.com</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="12838919517720373588" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText13511482680796016268" style="display:none">A wonderful, poetic book about love and memory. <div id="review_2139461475" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2139461475" />
    <a title="Paula" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16174645-paula"><img alt="Paula" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605390346p2/16174645.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2139461475?book_show_action=true">Feb 18, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paula" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paula" href="/user/show/16174645-paula">Paula</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9781433261503" href="/book/show/5440509-the-secret-scripture">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Irish literature fans, literary fans</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Paula by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/3845138-carol">Carol</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16174645-paula?shelf=ireland">ireland</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16174645-paula?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16174645-paula?shelf=favorite-authors">favorite-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16174645-paula?shelf=costa-book-award">costa-book-award</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16174645-paula?shelf=booker-prize-nominee">booker-prize-nominee</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16174645-paula?shelf=irish-book-award">irish-book-award</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16174645-paula?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16174645-paula?shelf=cultural">cultural</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16174645-paula?shelf=favorite-books">favorite-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16174645-paula?shelf=audio-book">audio-book</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2139461475" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8201998902174613655">I was first introduced to Sebastian Barry with Days Without End published in 2016 and winner of the Costa Book Award and a Booker nominee. Days Without End hit me like a ton of bricks...so fabulously written and such a tale. Did I ever imagine that another of his books could become so beloved? Well the Secret Scripture has. But it also made me REAL ANGRY.<br/><br/>Set after the Irish Civil War, Roseanne Clear McNulty is about to turn 100 years old and wants to tell her story. Roseanne has spent most of he</span>
  <span id="freeText8201998902174613655" style="display:none">I was first introduced to Sebastian Barry with Days Without End published in 2016 and winner of the Costa Book Award and a Booker nominee. Days Without End hit me like a ton of bricks...so fabulously written and such a tale. Did I ever imagine that another of his books could become so beloved? Well the Secret Scripture has. But it also made me REAL ANGRY.<br /><br />Set after the Irish Civil War, Roseanne Clear McNulty is about to turn 100 years old and wants to tell her story. Roseanne has spent most of her adult life confined to a psychiatric hospital in Ireland under tragic circumstances. She is the victim of Father Gaunt, the Irish Catholic priest of her village. Condemned for only talking to another man other than her new husband, the priest has made her out to be an adulteress and she looses everything. <br /><br />Father Gaunt represents everything I hate about the Catholic Church. The power priests in Ireland had over women, the abuse, the repression of sexuality and its people. As a non-practicing Irish Catholic I get so angry and disturbed by these fanatics. I hate shelf-righteous religious people, always black or white, always their way as there is no other. Their opinion is the only one that exists. I understand this so well as I was brought up in a born-again Catholic family. <br /><br />Sebastian Barry tells this heart-wrenching story so well. The Secret Scripture, which also won the 2008 Costa Award and Booker Prize nomination, is beautifully written and so poetic. The ending was quite unexpected! I listened to the audio as the narrator’s brogue is so delightful. <br /><br />Barry, an Irish playwright, novelist, and poet has become one of my favorite authors. Recently he took over the title of Irish Poet Laureate from Anne Enwright another of Ireland’s fabulous authors.<br /><br />If you are interested in the troubled history of Ireland, the Catholic Church’s repression of their society, or just reading a novel by one of the finest authors living, don’t miss.<br /><br />Highly recommend.<br />5 out of 5 stars</span>
  <a data-text-id="8201998902174613655" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1948192551" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1948192551" />
    <a title="BlackOxford" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17744555-blackoxford"><img alt="BlackOxford" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484863606p2/17744555.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1948192551?book_show_action=true">Mar 30, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="BlackOxford" class="user" itemprop="url" name="BlackOxford" href="/user/show/17744555-blackoxford">BlackOxford</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17744555-blackoxford?shelf=irish">irish</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1948192551" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1940753561008908132"><b>Innocent Betrayals</b><br/><br/>Secret Scripture is a story of betrayals - by those we love most, of them in turn by us; but particularly our betrayal of ourselves in memory and history. We betray ourselves through memories in which we both find and avoid guilt. We are innocent because we are hapless when it comes to memory. They are of us but neither reliable nor controllable by us. Memories rarely comfort. Good ones remind us of loss; bad ones evoke regret. Curiously, memories become dissociated from motive</span>
  <span id="freeText1940753561008908132" style="display:none"><b>Innocent Betrayals</b><br /><br />Secret Scripture is a story of betrayals - by those we love most, of them in turn by us; but particularly our betrayal of ourselves in memory and history. We betray ourselves through memories in which we both find and avoid guilt. We are innocent because we are hapless when it comes to memory. They are of us but neither reliable nor controllable by us. Memories rarely comfort. Good ones remind us of loss; bad ones evoke regret. Curiously, memories become dissociated from motives. So the reasons for our actions at best appear incomprehensible; at worst we end up condemning ourselves.<br /><br />According to Barry's fiction we don't calculate consequences - either of betraying or of being betrayed - we creep into situations which explode. We did not intend these explosions which destroy the matrix of life. They are beyond our control. We are then trapped in the rubble - of marriage, of family for an individual; and, for a community or a nation, of the enemies we have created of one other. Roseanne Clear, a centenarian confined in a mental hospital for seventy years is one such hapless victim - a woman cheated of her life through hatefulness and the mendacity of those closest to her. <br /><br />Roseanne, and her home of Sligo, also represent all of Ireland of the last century. Her "life spans everything, she is as much as we can know of our world, the last hundred years of it... The fact is we are missing so many threads in our Irish story that the tapestry of Irish life cannot but fall apart. There is nothing to hold it together." Barry describes a drear and confused Ireland, a land of religiosity without moral principles; populated by self-righteous priests and their repressed and obedient congregations. A land of fanatical peasants and their murderous leaders who have always blamed others for their murders, particularly the English whom they murdered as much as their own.<br /><br />But this description is also recognised as questionable by Barry. It is a judgement based on history. History is merely recorded memories and cannot be trusted. As the doctor in charge of Roseanne’s care comes to recognise, "I am beginning to wonder strongly what is the nature of history... most truth and fact offered by syntactical means is treacherous and unreliable." The unreliability is not so much down to lies as incompleteness, axes to be ground, loyalties to be safeguarded. Penetrating this morass seems impossible, but it sometimes can be done. Reality is then found "like a lost shilling on a floor of mud, glistening in some despair."<br /><br />This is a highly emotional book. It conjures sympathy, disgust, and ultimately hope in about equal measure. It is honest rather than clever; it is spare without being sparse. It is very Irish; and it is very good.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1940753561008908132" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2650292674" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2650292674" />
    <a title="Violet wells" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4196681-violet-wells"><img alt="Violet wells" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442260743p2/4196681.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2650292674?book_show_action=true">Jan 21, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Violet wells" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Violet wells" href="/user/show/4196681-violet-wells">Violet wells</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2650292674" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18196663151405845545">"History, as far as I can see, is not the arrangement of what happens, but a fabulous arrangement of surmises and guesses held up as a banner against the assault of withering truth." <br/><br/>People often speculate on how history might be told had the voices of women not been silenced. Barry here gives us the written testament of a Presbyterian working class woman who has reached her hundredth year and has been incarcerated in an asylum for decades. The Irish troubles are never overtly centre stage but t</span>
  <span id="freeText18196663151405845545" style="display:none">"History, as far as I can see, is not the arrangement of what happens, but a fabulous arrangement of surmises and guesses held up as a banner against the assault of withering truth." <br /><br />People often speculate on how history might be told had the voices of women not been silenced. Barry here gives us the written testament of a Presbyterian working class woman who has reached her hundredth year and has been incarcerated in an asylum for decades. The Irish troubles are never overtly centre stage but the fateful shockwaves they cause in the form of concussions and cleavages are a pivotal and malignant part of her history. Roseanne's testament to her life is juxtaposed with the musings of the consulting doctor of the asylum who takes an interest in Roseanne after his wife dies. <br /><br />Roseanne's story as told by herself is perhaps no more or no less trustworthy than any account an individual might provide of his or her own life. She is continually correcting herself, stopping up short, changing the subject, picking up the thread of a narrative and then dropping it. Her memories trouble her and will not be easily hammered into simple storylines. Barry ingeniously and progressively allows us to read between her lyrical and elusive lines. The truth she is seeking to tell about her life is not so much factual truth as emotional truth. To some extent she is mythologizing her life. She does this in a voice that possesses a beautiful lyrical strangeness that conjures up poignantly the poetic realm of experience - the secret scripture. The reader meanwhile is asking two pivotal and compelling questions - who is she writing this testament for and how did she spend most of her life in an asylum? Dr Grene's musings, called in the novel "Dr Grene's Commonplace Book", will eventually help provide answers to both questions, though he too, for guilty reasons of his own, initially seeks to hide as much as to reveal. When we finally learn the truth we are left musing on the culpability of masculinity, sometimes reinforced by female slavishness to convention, including of a religious nature, in not only simplifying history but perverting it. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18196663151405845545" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1298608446" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1298608446" />
    <a title="Jen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27709782-jen"><img alt="Jen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592393550p2/27709782.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1298608446?book_show_action=true">Jun 04, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jen" href="/user/show/27709782-jen">Jen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1298608446" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12242446136042424489">The Secret Scriptures is a remarkable holy grail of writing. <br/>Barry masterfully writes a poetic psychological mystery where he magically weaves a story of 2 voices: One of Roseanne McNulty, who now sits in a mental institution and has for the past 40 odd years of her 100 year life; and the other of her psychiatrist, Dr Grene, who has known her for the duration of her stay. Roseanne sits in her room as she nears the end of her life, reflecting back through the daily journal she writes and hides in</span>
  <span id="freeText12242446136042424489" style="display:none">The Secret Scriptures is a remarkable holy grail of writing. <br />Barry masterfully writes a poetic psychological mystery where he magically weaves a story of 2 voices: One of Roseanne McNulty, who now sits in a mental institution and has for the past 40 odd years of her 100 year life; and the other of her psychiatrist, Dr Grene, who has known her for the duration of her stay. Roseanne sits in her room as she nears the end of her life, reflecting back through the daily journal she writes and hides in the floorboards of her room. Grene has been told he needs to release patients as the hospital is closing down. He now has the task of evaluating those who are mentally sound and for reasons unbeknownst to him, were incarcerated for reasons other than mental illness. As such was the life of Roseanne - when the Irish Catholic church had a vice like grip on what morality was and for many women who didn’t fit the mold were demoralized and removed from society by being institutionalized often under horrific conditions. <br />Themes of good vs evil rebound; infidelity; aging; religion and loyalty. An eloquent read. 5 ★</span>
  <a data-text-id="12242446136042424489" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1318856566" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1318856566" />
    <a title="Kalliope" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3593962-kalliope"><img alt="Kalliope" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1428482885p2/3593962.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1318856566?book_show_action=true">Jun 27, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kalliope" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kalliope" href="/user/show/3593962-kalliope">Kalliope</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780571215294" href="/book/show/5101390-the-secret-scripture">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=2015">2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=ireland">ireland</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=fiction-english">fiction-english</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1318856566" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11306937665153982460"><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1436467017i/15463997._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Reading this novel I have felt as if I were peeling two onions: one yellow, one purple. First one, then the other, and back to the first and so on. My illusion was that after peeling its outer tunic and I proceeded to remove, slowly and gradually each scale leaf, I was lifting a veil and approaching the inner bud, a hidden core. The truth. <br/><br/>The yellow onion has less thinner and finer leaves. In their frailty and subtler delicacy of colour, they are as the veiled and vulnerable memories of an old </span>
  <span id="freeText11306937665153982460" style="display:none"><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1436467017i/15463997._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Reading this novel I have felt as if I were peeling two onions: one yellow, one purple. First one, then the other, and back to the first and so on. My illusion was that after peeling its outer tunic and I proceeded to remove, slowly and gradually each scale leaf, I was lifting a veil and approaching the inner bud, a hidden core. The truth. <br><br>The yellow onion has less thinner and finer leaves. In their frailty and subtler delicacy of colour, they are as the veiled and vulnerable memories of an old woman. Evocative and reminiscent. The purple one has thicker, heftier, almost corpulent scale leaves. They are also brighter in their colour contrast, as if written in black and white. In their matter-of-factness they seem manlier and have more purpose. As scientific men often do.<br><br>And then as I peel them both, they gradually begin to lose their distinctiveness, and become more alike. The white translucent tone predominates, and begins to feel as if they were the same onion after all.<br><br>This illusion or tale of two onions is deceptive. There is no such thing really. I am not peeling any onions. I have just read a novel. But I have imagined it but as I develop it the image seems to have acquired a greater consistency and reality than the book I have read. It seem to be getting all confused. Once I started with the onions simile my review has taken a flight of its own and I feel I am moving away from the book I have just read and am trying to review.<br><br>For example, the tale of the onions does not have any reference to the murky and violent politics as those suffered in the Ireland of the 1920s when the country endured the worst kind of wars--a civil one. It is certainly far away from any meditation of the thorny aspects of sexuality when there may be abuse or self-delusion involved. It ignores completely the issues of stale religions and conventions when they exclude individuals out of a fossilized set of social arrangements. For it does not include any consideration of the themes of betrayal and trust and how they combine with love, and what happens to broken dreams and candid idealisms under the pressure of torturing creeds. My invented analogy overlooks the peculiar mixture of crystal clear writing with elusive and elegant passages of lyricism with no sugar of Barry’s pen. And it omits to consider that one of the characters could be understood in its symbolic dimension and does not see that it is as big as an entire land.<br><br>But then the onions do present a story too, and we all need stories, for stories are the stuff of life and of our self-knowledge. So, yes, after all, Barry’s novel does have for me some aspects, even if somewhat reduced and simplified, of the story of my imagined peeling of the two onions, since the image did occur to me intricately connected to my reading of the novel. There must be something there, or at least in my mind, which after all is all that matters. At least to me.<br><br>And now as the imagined peeled onions get more and more entangled with my memory of Barry’s novel, I realize that someone who has not read the novel would have a difficult time ascertaining what the Secret Scripture is all about, since my own telling is just misconstruing and is disfigured by other ideas that populate my mind. And for those who have read it, my bringing in this stupid account of the onions, and my peeling of them, will appear as a mangled distortion.<br><br>At least let me say that I do not intend to trivialize Barry’s novel. Forget the onions. I should have concentrated on the actual process of unveiling: the continuous progress when moving from the distinct to the sameness, or from appearances to the essences. What may seem different may turn out to be of the same nature.<br><br>And yet, do not belittle banality. The games that the mind plays sometimes act as a defence against sorrow and despair.<br><br>Leaves of the onion, leaves of the book. Both could make you feel vulnerable and cry.<br></span>
  <a data-text-id="11306937665153982460" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Diane S ☔" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4159922-diane-s"><img alt="Diane S ☔" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598402138p2/4159922.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/383960437?book_show_action=true">Aug 04, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diane S ☔" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diane S ☔" href="/user/show/4159922-diane-s">Diane S ☔</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780670019403" href="/book/show/3215722-the-secret-scripture">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer383960437" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1331981096296753456">The Catholic Church was all powerful, a time in Ireland when religious and political factions cause almost unceasing distress and death. A young beautiful woman, a protestant woman, dares to fall in love with a Catholic but will end up spending a great part of her one-hundred years inside a psychiatric institution. Why and how did this happen?<br/><br/>A story written down by a very old woman, an account of the priest uncovered by Doctor Grene who is charged with discovering which of the residents, patien</span>
  <span id="freeText1331981096296753456" style="display:none">The Catholic Church was all powerful, a time in Ireland when religious and political factions cause almost unceasing distress and death. A young beautiful woman, a protestant woman, dares to fall in love with a Catholic but will end up spending a great part of her one-hundred years inside a psychiatric institution. Why and how did this happen?<br /><br />A story written down by a very old woman, an account of the priest uncovered by Doctor Grene who is charged with discovering which of the residents, patients would be allowed to go free when this old institution is destroyed. Yet, the accounts vary greater, so which one to believe?<br /><br />And so slowly unravels the story of a life, a time when a priest's word was unquestionable. Irish mothers and their sons, I married one, the son, well possibly the mother too. A very dense and wordy book, a book one must read slowly, a very sad and poignant tale. A very good and touching tale, that brings that whole period back to life and shows us the true story of what actually happened to Roseanne, and what Doctor Grene will decide to do in the face of the truth.<br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1331981096296753456" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText4945946955281031625" style="display:none"> “History as far as I can see, it’s not the arrangement of what happens, but a fabulous arrangement of surmises and guesses held up as a banner against the assault of the withering truth”. <br /><br />A deeply emotional heartbreaking story....<br />My heart was aching! <br />Painfully sad! <br /><br />Love, loss, loneliness, victimized injustice, betrayal, prejudice, Catholic Church repression.....<br />tragedy....upon tragedy!! <br /><br />This story with it’s surprising ending won’t be easy to shake. I couldn’t be the only reader who felt anger - mixed with grief.....<br />......quite an affecting novel —(one of the best written books I’ve ever read within a mental institution)....<br /> ....with two forever- memorable protagonists! <br /><br />Spectacular beautiful prose. <br /><br />Note: there are many top notch reviews that have been written: But, in my opinion, this is a great book to go into blind—<br />THEN.... <br />.....enjoy the great many reviews and the discussions you involve yourself with. <br /><br />Many thanks to the Goodreads community for steering me in the direction of this novel! <br /><br />Many thanks for the fantastic reviews from Candi, Dem, Diane, Cecily, Sara’, Violet, Blair, Cheri, Annet, Debra, Paula, Manny, and others....<br /><br />I’m glad I finally took a turn!!! <br /><br /><br /></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2937012665" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13004729380436497477">I really wish Goodreads offered the chance to award half-stars. This was my second Sebastian Barry novel, after Days Without End, which I read back in 2018. While TSS wasn't quite poor enough to gain three stars, DWE wasn't good enough to gain five, and so, despite enjoying one far less than the other, both get four stars. It hardly seems fair, really.<br/><br/>Incidentally, this would have earned the full four stars if I hadn't guessed the 'surprise twist' at precisely the 31% mark (this despite the fact</span>
  <span id="freeText13004729380436497477" style="display:none">I really wish Goodreads offered the chance to award half-stars. This was my second Sebastian Barry novel, after Days Without End, which I read back in 2018. While TSS wasn't quite poor enough to gain three stars, DWE wasn't good enough to gain five, and so, despite enjoying one far less than the other, both get four stars. It hardly seems fair, really.<br /><br />Incidentally, this would have earned the full four stars if I hadn't guessed the 'surprise twist' at precisely the 31% mark (this despite the fact I'm notoriously stupid when it comes to plot twists). The prose was also slightly overdone in places, though all in all I'm rather in love with Barry's writing.</span>
